Published by Elsevier B.V.</prism:copyright><prism:publisher>The Authors. Published by Elsevier B.V.</prism:publisher><dc:creator>Aina, Sergio</dc:creator><dc:creator>Tratnik, Blaz</dc:creator><dc:creator>Vizintin, Alen</dc:creator><dc:creator>Tchernychova, Elena</dc:creator><dc:creator>Lobera, M. Pilar</dc:creator><dc:creator>Dominko, Robert</dc:creator><dc:creator>Bernechea, María</dc:creator><dc:description>
                  Hard carbons are the most extended anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs); however, they suffer from several limitations such as low stability, poor rate performance and low initial Coulombic efficiency (iCE). Herein, a simple, fast, and low-cost surface treatment at room temperature using short-chain organic molecules: 3-mercaptopropionic acid (MPA), 1,2-ethanedithiol (EDT) and oxalic acid (OxA) has been applied to a hard carbon (C1400).
                  The carbons treated with sulfur containing molecules (MPA or EDT) exhibit higher capacity (12 % capacity enhancement after 100th cycles at C/10 and 18 % enhancement at 1C vs. C1400). The introduction of these ligands leads to improved micropore blockage, helping in the reversible insertion of Na ions. Moreover, ex-situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analyses demonstrate that thiol functional groups promote the formation of favorable NaF and Na2O-rich solid electrolyte interfaces (SEI) leading to and faster sodium diffusion in the plateau region. Additionally, MPA and EDT treatments have been applied to a soft carbon (Vulcan XC-72R) resulting in a substantial 30 % capacity improvement after 100 cycles at 1C.
                  These results demonstrate the wide applicability of the method as a straightforward and efficient strategy for improving the electrochemical properties of carbon anodes used in SIBs.

            <ce:date-received day="12" month="2" year="2024"/>
            <ce:date-revised day="26" month="4" year="2024"/>
            <ce:date-accepted day="13" month="5" year="2024"/>
            <ce:abstract xml:lang="en" id="abs0010" view="all" class="author"><ce:abstract-sec id="abssec0010" view="all">
                  <ce:simple-para id="abspara0010" view="all">Hard carbons are the most extended anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs); however, they suffer from several limitations such as low stability, poor rate performance and low initial Coulombic efficiency (iCE). Herein, a simple, fast, and low-cost surface treatment at room temperature using short-chain organic molecules: 3-mercaptopropionic acid (MPA), 1,2-ethanedithiol (EDT) and oxalic acid (OxA) has been applied to a hard carbon (C1400).</ce:simple-para>
                  <ce:simple-para id="abspara0015" view="all">The carbons treated with sulfur containing molecules (MPA or EDT) exhibit higher capacity (12 % capacity enhancement after 100th cycles at C/10 and 18 % enhancement at 1C vs. C1400). The introduction of these ligands leads to improved micropore blockage, helping in the reversible insertion of Na ions. Moreover, <ce:italic>ex-situ</ce:italic> X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analyses demonstrate that thiol functional groups promote the formation of favorable NaF and Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O-rich solid electrolyte interfaces (SEI) leading to and faster sodium diffusion in the plateau region. Additionally, MPA and EDT treatments have been applied to a soft carbon (Vulcan XC-72R) resulting in a substantial 30 % capacity improvement after 100 cycles at 1C.</ce:simple-para>
                  <ce:simple-para id="abspara0020" view="all">These results demonstrate the wide applicability of the method as a straightforward and efficient strategy for improving the electrochemical properties of carbon anodes used in SIBs.</ce:simple-para>
               </ce:abstract-sec></ce:abstract>
            <ce:abstract class="graphical" id="abs0015" view="all">
               <ce:section-title id="sectitle0015">Graphical abstract</ce:section-title>
               <ce:abstract-sec id="abssec0015" view="all">
                  <ce:simple-para id="abspara0025" view="all">This study introduces an efficient and simple surface treatment with short chain ligands that blocks micropores, improves sodium diffusion, and increases capacity of carbons used as anodes in sodium-ion batteries. Ligands with thiol functional groups promote a NaF and Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O-rich SEI, leading to anodes with improved capacity and stability.<ce:display>
                        <ce:figure id="undfig1">
                           <ce:alt-text role="short" id="alttext0010">Image 1</ce:alt-text>
                           <ce:link locator="ga1" xlink:type="simple" xlink:role="http://data.elsevier.com/vocabulary/ElsevierContentTypes/23.4" xlink:href="pii:S0378775324006827/ga1"/>
                        </ce:figure>
                     </ce:display>
                  </ce:simple-para>
               </ce:abstract-sec>
            </ce:abstract>

         <body view="all">
            <ce:sections>
               <ce:section id="sec1" role="introduction" view="all">
                  <ce:label>1</ce:label>
                  <ce:section-title id="sectitle0030">Introduction</ce:section-title>
                  <ce:para id="p0035" view="all">The pursuit of clean energy generation and efficient energy storage systems has become imperative in our society. As the global demand for energy continues to rise, there is a growing need to transition from fossil fuel-based power sources to cleaner alternatives. Therefore, renewable energy sources integration has become the focus of many countries and organizations as they have the potential to reduce greenhouse emissions and fossil fuel dependency. However, one of the primary challenges of renewable energy lies in its storage capabilities. Unlike fossil fuels, that can provide instant energy when required, renewable sources are reliant on climate conditions, resulting in occasional energy peaks and fluctuations in energy generation, including both oversupplies and undersupplies [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib1" id="crosref0070">1</ce:cross-ref>]. To address these challenges the development of efficient energy storage systems has become critical. Nowadays lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are the most used systems in portable electronics and are considered a highly important storage system for short and intermittent energy storage due to their high energy density and design flexibility. Nevertheless, despite their widespread use, they have well-known drawbacks. These limitations include scarcity and limited geographic distribution of key materials like lithium or cobalt, safety issues, and the formation of dendrites [<ce:cross-refs refid="bib2 bib3 bib4" id="crosrefs0010">2–4</ce:cross-refs>]. In recent years sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) gain a significant interest due to the abundance of sodium, the potential cost-effectiveness, and environmental acceptance. However, one of the major challenges is the development of an anode material able to effectively store sodium ions. While graphite is commonly used as the anode material in standard rechargeable LIBs, it is inadequate for SIBs, due to its inability to form binary compounds with sodium ions [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib5" id="crosref0075">5</ce:cross-ref>]. As a result, the identification and development of alternative anode materials for SIBs has become a priority in the field of electrochemical storage research.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0040" view="all">In this context, amorphous carbons such as hard carbons (HCs) and soft carbons (SCs), have risen as the most common option for anodes in SIBs. Their low-cost, and environmentally friendly synthesis combined with great storage capacity have placed them in the spotlight in recent years. HCs are currently the preferred anode in commercial SIBs (for example, CATL, Tiamat, and Faradion batteries); however, SIBs using SCs anode are also being commercialized (HiNA Battery) [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib6" id="crosref0080">6</ce:cross-ref>]. HCs are mainly obtained from the carbonization of biomass-based precursors. They exhibit great capacity values that can reach 300 mAh·g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>, mainly due to their low-potential plateau below 0.1 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> [<ce:cross-refs refid="bib7 bib8 bib9 bib10" id="crosrefs0015">7–10</ce:cross-refs>]. However, this also results in poor rate performance and low stability [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib11" id="crosref0085">11</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib12" id="crosref0090">12</ce:cross-ref>]. In contrast to HCs, SCs are obtained from high-temperature pyrolysis of aromatic compounds. They exhibit a sloping region during both the sodiation and desodiation processes, lacking a low-potential plateau, which results in enhanced stability and better high-rate performance. However, their average capacities tend to be lower than those of hard carbons oscillating between 100 and 250 mAh·g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup> [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib9" id="crosref0095">9</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib11" id="crosref0100">11</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib12" id="crosref0105">12</ce:cross-ref>]. The differences in their sodium storage mechanisms arise from their nanometric structure, where SCs exhibit less curvature and a higher degree of order in the stacking of their graphene layers [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib12" id="crosref0110">12</ce:cross-ref>].</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0045" view="all">One of the most important concepts in the anode performance is the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) which is crucial for the electrochemistry of sodium ion batteries (SIBs) anodes due to its influence on sodium transport and stability. The term SEI was first introduced in 1979 by <ce:italic>Peled</ce:italic> when he observed a layer formation on alkali metal upon contact with organic electrolytes [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib13" id="crosref0115">13</ce:cross-ref>]. This layer was found to be ionically conductive but nonconductive electronically. In 1997, <ce:italic>Peled</ce:italic> et al. proposed the “mosaic model” as the prevailing structure, which continues to be widely accepted in present times [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib14" id="crosref0120">14</ce:cross-ref>]. This structure comprises small domains of inorganic, organic salts and polymers which allow the transfer of the ions (Li, Na, K …) and protects the surface of the anode [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib15" id="crosref0125">15</ce:cross-ref>]. These species are formed upon decomposition of the organic electrolyte on the anode surface during the first cycle. This process is influenced by the nature of the organic solvents, electrolyte salt, additives, and functional groups on the anode surface [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib15" id="crosref0130">15</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib16" id="crosref0135">16</ce:cross-ref>]. Unfortunately, hard carbons and soft carbons usually form inhomogeneous SEI layers with commercial electrolytes which lead to low initial Coulombic efficiency (iCE), low cycling stability, and poor rate capability [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib17" id="crosref0140">17</ce:cross-ref>]. SEI engineering and interface chemistry regulation have risen as possible solutions to improve the LIBs and SIBs performance [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib18" id="crosref0145">18</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib19" id="crosref0150">19</ce:cross-ref>]. Different approaches have been proposed to tune the SEI such as: different electrolyte solvents [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib20" id="crosref0155">20</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib21" id="crosref0160">21</ce:cross-ref>], electrolyte additives [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib16" id="crosref0165">16</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib22" id="crosref0170">22</ce:cross-ref>], oxygen plasma functionalization [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib23" id="crosref0175">23</ce:cross-ref>], and atomic layer deposition (ALD) [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib24" id="crosref0180">24</ce:cross-ref>]. Another common approach is the coating of HC anodes with SC or preparing HC-SC composites to block open pores, reduce surface area and therefore, decrease the SEI decomposition to enhance the iCE [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib25" id="crosref0185">25</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib26" id="crosref0190">26</ce:cross-ref>]. Special attention has been placed on introducing oxygen functional groups on the surface of the carbon anode via chemical oxidation [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib27" id="crosref0195">27</ce:cross-ref>] or <ce:italic>in-situ</ce:italic> polymerization of carbonyl-rich molecules to improve wettability and act as active sites for electrolyte decomposition [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib17" id="crosref0200">17</ce:cross-ref>]. Additionally, carbon functionalization using molecules has also been explored. <ce:italic>Romero-Cano</ce:italic> et al. [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib28" id="crosref0205">28</ce:cross-ref>] proposed the incorporation of N-rich molecules (urea and melamine) to homogenize the surface chemistry of hard carbons, preventing irreversibility and providing extra capacity storage mechanisms. <ce:italic>Yu</ce:italic> et al. [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib29" id="crosref0210">29</ce:cross-ref>] modified the surface of an HC using 2,2-dimethylvinyl boric acid (DEBA) to construct a more stable SEI achieving an iCE of 77 % and 90 % capacity retention after 2000 cycles in comparison to the 65 % iCE and 54 % capacity retention of unmodified hard carbon. These surface treatments have shown to improve the capacity, stability, and iCE of pristine carbons; however, most of them require complex techniques, high temperatures, long treatment times, or modifying the synthesis and pyrolysis steps of the carbon.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0050" view="all">Two of the most used solvents in SIBs are ethylene carbonate (EC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC) due to their high dielectric constant, low viscosity, and high ionic mobility. They undergo decomposition upon contact with the anode during the initial sodiation and desodiation cycles mainly forming Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>, Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O and several polycarbonates [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib20" id="crosref0215">20</ce:cross-ref>]. Additionally, additives such as fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) and the NaPF<ce:inf loc="post">6</ce:inf> salt decompose to form NaF [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib16" id="crosref0220">16</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib30" id="crosref0225">30</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib31" id="crosref0230">31</ce:cross-ref>]. It has been reported that a high amount of carbonates in the SEI is detrimental to SIB performance [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib32" id="crosref0235">32</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib33" id="crosref0240">33</ce:cross-ref>]. On the other hand, other inorganic components of SEI such as Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O and NaF possess higher Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> conductivity and migration than organic compounds, which induces better rate capability [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib17" id="crosref0245">17</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib32" id="crosref0250">32</ce:cross-ref>], provides high mechanical strength [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib34" id="crosref0255">34</ce:cross-ref>], and suppresses the growth of Na dendrites leading to better stability [<ce:cross-refs refid="bib35 bib36 bib37" id="crosrefs0020">35–37</ce:cross-refs>].</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0055" view="all">In this work we propose a simple, fast, and low-cost surface treatment at room temperature using short-chain organic molecules: 3-mercaptopropionic acid (MPA), 1,2-ethanedithiol (EDT) and oxalic acid (OxA). They are short-chain ligands of similar size containing functional groups such as thiols (MPA and EDT) and carboxylic acids (MPA and OxA), which can enhance the interaction between the electrode surface and electrolyte, and modify the composition of the SEI [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib38" id="crosref0260">38</ce:cross-ref>]. In a previous study, we observed a notable enhancement in the wettability, capacity, and stability of an aqueous supercapacitor electrode upon the introduction of the short-chain organic molecule MPA [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib39" id="crosref0265">39</ce:cross-ref>]. However, a deeper study on the reasons behind these improvements was not performed. In this study, we have studied the effect of having different functional groups on SEI formation, with special emphasis on the formation of carbonates (CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>
                     <ce:sup loc="post">2−</ce:sup>), Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O and NaF contents and their influence on electrochemistry. A corncob-derived carbon (C1400) has been chosen as a hard carbon (HC) model. This hard carbon has proven to be very promising for Na-ion battery anodes, but it exhibited problems with ion blockage related to SEI formation [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib40" id="crosref0270">40</ce:cross-ref>]. Given the positive impact of the surface treatments on the hard carbon performance, the best performing treatments have been applied to Vulcan XC-72R (chosen as the soft carbon model), showing a more noticeable capacity improvement. These results demonstrate the versatility and wide applicability of the method for both HCs and SCs.</ce:para>
               </ce:section>
               <ce:section id="sec2" role="materials-methods" view="all">
                  <ce:label>2</ce:label>
                  <ce:section-title id="sectitle0035">Experimental (materials and methods)</ce:section-title>
                  <ce:section id="sec2.1" view="all">
                     <ce:label>2.1</ce:label>
                     <ce:section-title id="sectitle0040">Materials</ce:section-title>
                     <ce:para id="p0060" view="all">Organic ligands 3-mercaptopropionic acid (MPA), 1,2-ethanedithiol (EDT), and oxalic acid (OxA) were purchased from Sigma Aldrich. Toluene (&gt;98 %), and pure acetonitrile were purchased from VWR Chemicals. H<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>SO<ce:inf loc="post">4</ce:inf> (&gt;98 %) was acquired from ACRO Organics.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0065" view="all">Polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) (M<ce:inf loc="post">w</ce:inf> ∼ 534,000 (GPC)), sodium chunks, ethylene carbonate (EC), and dimethyl carbonate (DMC) were purchased from Sigma Aldrich. Sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<ce:inf loc="post">6</ce:inf>), and fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) were purchased from Alfa Aesar. Super C65 conductive carbon was purchased from Imerys, N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) was obtained from Merck, Carbon-coated aluminum foil was acquired from Armor, France, and Vulcan XC-72R was kindly provided by CABOT corporation.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0070" view="all">Corncob was obtained from a local field. Preceding the pyrolysis process, the corncob underwent an overnight drying procedure in a dryer maintained at 80 °C. The initial heat treatment was performed within a quartz tube furnace, exposed to a flow of argon (15 L h<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>), during which the temperature was elevated to 900 °C at a rate of 2.5 °C·min<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>. Upon reaching 900 °C, the temperature was sustained for a duration of 1 h before gradual cooling to room temperature. Subsequently, the second phase entailed elevating temperature to 1400 °C using an Al<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf> tube furnace, while maintaining an argon flow (50 L h<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>). The heating rate was fixed at 2.5 °C·min<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>, and the temperature was held constant for 1 h, followed by cooling to room temperature. After the pyrolysis, the obtained corncob C1400 hard carbon was grinded [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib40" id="crosref0275">40</ce:cross-ref>].</ce:para>
                  </ce:section>
                  <ce:section id="sec2.2" view="all">
                     <ce:label>2.2</ce:label>
                     <ce:section-title id="sectitle0045">Surface treatments</ce:section-title>
                     <ce:para id="p0075" view="all">Organic ligand solutions (MPA 1 M, EDT 1 M, and OxA 0.1 M) were prepared by dissolving MPA, EDT, or OxA in acetonitrile or in an acidic acetonitrile solution (0.1 M H<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>SO<ce:inf loc="post">4</ce:inf> in acetonitrile). Two sets of surface-treated samples were prepared with sulfuric acid (CA-L) and without (C-L) to analyze the effect of including the acid. Corncob 1400 °C powder was thoroughly mixed with freshly prepared organic ligand solutions (1 mL of solution per 100 mg of carbon) for 30 min under vigorous stirring. Afterward, samples were washed several times with acetonitrile and toluene and dried at 80 °C overnight. Samples were labeled as C1400 for the original hard carbon material, and C-MPA, C-EDT, and C-OxA for the surface treated samples without H<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>SO<ce:inf loc="post">4</ce:inf>. On the other hand, C1400 carbon treated with sulfuric acid was labeled as CA, CA-MPA, CA-EDT, and CA-OxA when H<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>SO<ce:inf loc="post">4</ce:inf> was included in the ligand solution.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0080" view="all">In the case of the soft carbon (Vulcan XC-72R) treatments, the treatment was slightly modified due to the higher porosity of the soft carbon. We added 2 mL of 0.5 M MPA or EDT solutions in acetonitrile per 100 mg of carbon. Samples were labeled as V-MPA or V-EDT.</ce:para>
                  </ce:section>
                  <ce:section id="sec2.3" view="all">
                     <ce:label>2.3</ce:label>
                     <ce:section-title id="sectitle0050">Electrode preparation and cell assembly</ce:section-title>
                     <ce:para id="p0085" view="all">Electrode slurries were prepared with a composition of 90 %wt. of active material (C1400, C-L, CA-L, Vulcan or V-L), 5 %wt. of PVDF binder, and a 5 %wt. of conductive carbon C65. NMP was used as a solvent. The mixture was ball milled at 300 rpm for 30 min to obtain a homogeneous slurry. The slurry was coated on Al/C foil using a doctor blade applicator resulting in a uniform coating with a thickness of 100 μm. The final coated material underwent an overnight drying process at 80 °C. Subsequently, electrodes with a diameter of 15 mm were cut and stored within an argon-filled glovebox, featuring mass loadings ranging between 1.5 and 2 mg cm<ce:sup loc="post">−2</ce:sup>.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0090" view="all">Two-electrode CR2032 type coin cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox with water and oxygen contents below 0.5 ppm. C1400, Vulcan, surface-treated C1400 or surface-treated Vulcan materials were used as working electrodes while sodium metal was used as the counter/reference electrode. Both electrodes were separated by a glass fiber separator. The electrolyte used was 1 M NaPF<ce:inf loc="post">6</ce:inf> dissolved in EC:DMC (1:1 %vol.) with 2 %wt. FEC additive. Each cell was carefully loaded with 100 μL of the electrolyte solution.</ce:para>
                  </ce:section>
                  <ce:section id="sec2.4" view="all">
                     <ce:label>2.4</ce:label>
                     <ce:section-title id="sectitle0055">Physical characterization</ce:section-title>
                     <ce:para id="p0095" view="all">N<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> adsorption measurements were performed using ASAP 2020 gas sorption analyzer from Micromeritics. The degassing step was performed at 120 °C. MicroActive software was used to process the data and obtain the Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) surface area by using Rouquerol procedure. Carbon slit pores by NLDFT model was used for the pore size distribution calculations.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0100" view="all">Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images and Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) analysis were performed in a SEM Inspect 50 instrument. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images were acquired using a Tecnai F30 microscope operating at 300 kV. Samples were prepared by sonicating carbon powders in ethanol and adding a couple of drops on holey-carbon copper grids. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was performed under 50 mL min<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup> N<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> flow, from 35 °C to 1000 °C at a heating rate of 10 °C·min<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup> on a TGA Q5000 IR from TA Instruments. Powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) was performed in an X-ray diffractometer PANalytical Empyrean instrument in Bragg-Brentano configuration using Cu Kα radiation and equipped with a PIXcel1D detector. Attenuated total reflection Fourier transform infrared (ATR-FTIR) spectra were obtained using a Bruker Vertex-70 FTIR spectrophotometer with a Golden Gate diamond ATR module. Data points were collected from 3500 cm<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup> to 1000 cm<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup> at a resolution of 4 cm<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup> and 512 number of scans. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) of carbon materials was performed with an Axis Supra spectrometer (Kratos Tech). <ce:italic>Ex-situ</ce:italic> X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) of cycled electrodes was performed using the Versa probe 3 AD (Physical Electronics, Chanhassen, U.S.A) with a monochromatic Al Kα X-ray source operating at 15 kV and 13.3 mA. Samples were subjected to cycling at a C/10 rate, comprising one discharge and one charge cycle. Subsequently, the half-cell was disassembled, and the HC electrode was meticulously cleaned using DMC prior to the analysis. Cycled electrodes were put on a non-conductive double-sided scotch tape in the “floating” setup and placed in the center of the XPS holder inside an argon filled glove box. The samples were transferred inside into the XPS intro chamber with a transfer vessel without air exposure. For each sample, spectra were acquired by scanning a 1 × 1 mm<ce:sup loc="post">2</ce:sup> area with the spot size of 200 μm. Narrow spectra were acquired at a pass energy of 27 eV and step of 0.05 eV to obtain high quality data. Analysis of the peaks was performed with the CasaXPS software using a weighted sum of Lorentzian and Gaussian component curves after Shirley background subtraction. The binding energies were referenced to the internal C1s standard at 284.6 eV.</ce:para>
                  </ce:section>
                  <ce:section id="sec2.5" view="all">
                     <ce:label>2.5</ce:label>
                     <ce:section-title id="sectitle0060">Electrochemical characterization</ce:section-title>
                     <ce:para id="p0105" view="all">The electrochemical characterization was performed by using Maccor Series 4200 or GAMRY equipment. Measurements consisted of charge–discharge C/10 galvanostatic cycling (100 cycles at 0.03 A g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>) or modified galvanostatic cycling (first 5 cycles at C/10 followed by 100 cycles at 1C or 0.3 A g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>). Cycling was performed in the voltage range between 2 V and 5 mV. Voltage hold was applied at the lower cut-off for 5 h or until the current dropped to C/100. The theoretical capacity was chosen following the model proposed by <ce:italic>Bommier</ce:italic> et al. [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib41" id="crosref0280">41</ce:cross-ref>].</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0110" view="all">Modified Galvanostatic Intermittent Titration Technique (GITT) measurements were performed at C/10 applied current during 30 min with 2 h of relaxation between steps. Apparent diffusion coefficients were calculated from Fick's second law and linear correlation assumptions [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib42" id="crosref0290">42</ce:cross-ref>]. C-rate performance measurements were conducted within a voltage window from 2 V to 0.005 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup>. The protocol included an initial stabilization cycle to form the SEI and 5 consecutive cycles with the current 30 mAh g-1 (C/10) with applied constant voltage at the lower cut-off until the current drops below C/100 or during a maximum of 5 h. Subsequently, 5 cycles at each respective current were performed. The time limits of the voltage hold were accordingly adjusted to the different C-rates (2.5 h for C/5, 1 h for C/2 …). Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy (EIS) were performed using a 2-electrode half-cell experimental configuration, using an amplitude of 10 mV across a frequency range from 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz. Circuit modeling and data processing was performed using the GAMRY Echem Analyst software.</ce:para>
                  </ce:section>
               </ce:section>
               <ce:section id="sec3" role="results" view="all">
                  <ce:label>3</ce:label>
                  <ce:section-title id="sectitle0065">Results and discussion</ce:section-title>
                  <ce:section id="sec3.1" view="all">
                     <ce:label>3.1</ce:label>
                     <ce:section-title id="sectitle0070">Physico-chemical characterization of surface-treated materials</ce:section-title>
                     <ce:para id="p0115" view="all">Surface-treated materials were prepared by simply mixing the carbon powder with the ligand solution under constant stirring at room temperature for 30 min. At the beginning, sulfuric acid was used for the preparation of the ligand solutions to enhance the bonding between ligands and hard carbon due to its catalytic properties, especially in reactions such as carboxylic acid esterification (Fisher esterification) [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib43" id="crosref0295">43</ce:cross-ref>]. But, it was observed that ligands were present on the surface even without acid treatment.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0120" view="all">XRD patterns show that surface treatments do not modify the C1400 crystalline structure (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig1" id="crosref0300">Fig. 1</ce:cross-ref>
                        <ce:float-anchor refid="fig1"/>a and b). The expected (002) and (100) carbon peaks are distinguished in all materials [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib40" id="crosref0305">40</ce:cross-ref>]. With regards to sulfuric acid pretreated samples, the emergence of new peaks was observed, which may be indicative of the formation of sulfate salts (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig1" id="crosref0310">Fig. 1</ce:cross-ref>a). This hypothesis was further supported by the disappearance of the peaks after washing the samples with water (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0315">Fig. S1</ce:cross-ref>). Moreover, C-OxA and CA-OxA show two common peaks at 26° and 30° which might correspond to solid oxalic acid [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib44" id="crosref0320">44</ce:cross-ref>]. All samples treated with carboxylic-ending molecules (OxA and MPA) show a new signal at 1680 cm<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup> in the ATR-FTIR spectra (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0325">Fig. S2</ce:cross-ref>), that is attributed to the C<ce:glyph name="dbnd"/>O stretching vibration of the carboxylic groups. The sample C-OxA shows the signal at 1705 cm<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup> (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0330">Fig. S2</ce:cross-ref>b) which corresponds to the second harmonic stretching of the same bond [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib45" id="crosref0335">45</ce:cross-ref>]. As expected, C1400, CA, CA-EDT or C-EDT samples show no signal at this wavenumber. No S–H stretching signal between 2600 and 2500 cm<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup> has been found for any of the samples probably due to the interaction of sulfur with carbon or deprotonation of the thiol group.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0125" view="all">TGA measurements have been performed to quantify and confirm the incorporation of the organic ligands on the carbon surface. Experiments were conducted in a nitrogen atmosphere to identify organic molecules adhered to the carbon surface and to differentiate variations in the strength of chemical bonding as a function of degradation temperature. Mass losses before 150 °C are attributed to adsorbed solvents and water [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib46" id="crosref0340">46</ce:cross-ref>]. Samples treated in acidic media are compared with sulfuric acid-treated carbon (CA) in <ce:cross-ref refid="fig1" id="crosref0345">Fig. 1</ce:cross-ref>c, and the surface treated samples are compared with the native carbon (C1400) in <ce:cross-ref refid="fig1" id="crosref0350">Fig. 1</ce:cross-ref>d. Mass losses in CA are attributed to the decomposition of surface functional groups and degradation of C–SO<ce:inf loc="post">x</ce:inf> generated by interaction with the acid (240 °C–350 °C) [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib47" id="crosref0355">47</ce:cross-ref>]. As expected, additional degradations are observed at 190 °C for CA-OxA, 230 °C for CA-EDT, and 200–235 °C for CA-MPA, attributed to molecules chemically bonded to the carbon through C–O and C–S bonds [<ce:cross-refs refid="bib46 bib47 bib48 bib49" id="crosrefs0025">46–49</ce:cross-refs>]. The difference in degradation temperature suggests that MPA and EDT are more strongly bound to the carbon than OxA. Moreover, CA-MPA and CA-EDT samples show an additional mass loss at 350 °C, which can indicate C–S interaction between thiols and carbon surface, or aliphatic carbons bonded to oxygen (C<ce:inf loc="post">al</ce:inf>–O) [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib47" id="crosref0360">47</ce:cross-ref>]. Non pretreated carbon (C1400, <ce:cross-ref refid="fig1" id="crosref0365">Fig. 1</ce:cross-ref>d) shows two mass losses at 330 °C and 450 °C due to the decomposition of functional surface groups. Besides, surface-treated materials show their first degradation at 205 °C for C-OxA, 250 °C for C-EDT and 282 °C for C-MPA with a shoulder at 230 °C. Again, MPA and EDT appear to be more strongly bonded to carbon than OxA. These results evidence the successful functionalization of the carbon with the three ligand molecules regardless of the presence of sulfuric acid in the treatment. Furthermore, loadings of the different ligands have been compiled in <ce:cross-ref refid="tbl1" id="crosref0370">Table 1</ce:cross-ref>
                        <ce:float-anchor refid="tbl1"/>. MPA and EDT loading is around 0.5 %wt. and 0.2 %wt. for OxA. The utilization of sulfuric acid seems to have a negligible impact on the quantity of incorporated ligands.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0130" view="all">TEM and SEM imaging shows that surface treatments have no visible impact on the topology of the carbon surface (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0375">Fig. S3</ce:cross-ref>). SEM-EDX semi quantitative analysis (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0380">Table S1</ce:cross-ref>) shows that all C1400 samples pretreated with sulfuric acid show at least 0.5 %wt. S content attributed to the presence of sulfates (further confirmed in the XPS analysis, <ce:cross-ref refid="fig2" id="crosref0385">Fig. 2</ce:cross-ref>
                        <ce:float-anchor refid="fig2"/>c). Moreover, C-MPA and C-EDT samples have around 0.5 %wt. S content attributed to the presence of MPA and EDT molecules. No sulfur is detected for C1400 or C-OxA. No significant differences in ligand loading are observed in the presence of sulfuric acid. Despite several washings, K and Si from lignocellulose precursors are still detected in the analysis [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib40" id="crosref0390">40</ce:cross-ref>].</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0135" view="all">XPS analyses have been performed to corroborate the incorporation of the ligand molecules onto the carbon surface. Elemental quantification is summarized in <ce:cross-ref refid="tbl2" id="crosref0395">Table 2</ce:cross-ref>
                        <ce:float-anchor refid="tbl2"/>. C <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic>, O <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> and S <ce:italic>2p</ce:italic> are the main narrow spectra that have been studied for this purpose.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0140" view="all">The C <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> peak fitting (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig2" id="crosref0400">Fig. 2</ce:cross-ref>a and b) shows the same three components for all the materials, corresponding to C–C/C<ce:glyph name="dbnd"/>C, C–O/C–S and C<ce:glyph name="dbnd"/>O from lower to higher binding energy (BE). For molecules with carboxylic acid groups (CA-MPA, CA-OxA, C-MPA, and C-OxA) an additional peak at around 289 eV, assigned to the presence of –COOH, has been observed. This signal is not detectable in the other samples. The peak fitting of O <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> spectra follows the same tendency (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0405">Fig. S4</ce:cross-ref>).</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0145" view="all">All materials show C<ce:glyph name="dbnd"/>O, C–OH/C–O–C signals and CA-MPA, CA-OxA, C-MPA, and C-OxA have an additional component at higher BE assigned to –COOH [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib50" id="crosref0410">50</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib51" id="crosref0415">51</ce:cross-ref>]. Both, C <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> and O <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic>, confirm the successful incorporation of OxA and MPA on the carbon surface. Besides, S <ce:italic>2p</ce:italic> narrow spectra were studied to confirm the presence of sulfur species in MPA and EDT samples (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig2" id="crosref0420">Fig. 2</ce:cross-ref>c). The presence of sulfates has been detected in all samples, with a comparatively higher concentration observed in samples pretreated with sulfuric acid. Moreover, C-MPA, CA-MPA, C-EDT, and CA-EDT display a characteristic <ce:italic>2p</ce:italic>
                        <ce:inf loc="post">
                           <ce:italic>3/2</ce:italic>
                        </ce:inf> signal at approximately 163.3 eV, which can be attributed to the presence of C–S–C bonds [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib52" id="crosref0425">52</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib53" id="crosref0430">53</ce:cross-ref>] thereby verifying the bonding between carbon and molecule thiol groups. Oppositely, as expected, C1400, CA, C-OxA, and CA-OxA do not exhibit such signals. In both cases, CA-L and C-L covalent bonding between carbon and molecule is detected regardless of the incorporation of sulfuric acid in the functionalization. These findings are consistent with the conclusions drawn from the TGA curves.</ce:para>
                  </ce:section>
                  <ce:section id="sec3.2" view="all">
                     <ce:label>3.2</ce:label>
                     <ce:section-title id="sectitle0075">Electrochemical characterization</ce:section-title>
                     <ce:para id="p0150" view="all">CA-L, C-L and pristine C1400 were used to fabricate electrodes for their testing in half-cell Na-ion batteries. Initially, CA-L samples were subjected to galvanostatic cycling at C/10, during 100 cycles (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig3" id="crosref0435">Fig. 3</ce:cross-ref>
                        <ce:float-anchor refid="fig3"/>a and <ce:cross-ref refid="tbl3" id="crosref0440">Table 3</ce:cross-ref>
                        <ce:float-anchor refid="tbl3"/>). A slight increase in capacity is observed for the surface-treated samples, with an 8 % capacity improvement for CA-MPA in the 100th cycle and a 6 % for CA-EDT and CA-OxA. It is noteworthy that the CA sample (C1400 pretreated only with sulfuric acid) shows a 2 % capacity improvement, which makes it very similar to C1400; however, it degrades faster than the other samples. Comparing the capacity in the 2nd cycle and 100th cycle, CA shows capacity retention of 84 %, lower than the 91 % observed for C1400 and CA-OxA, and 87 % for CA-EDT and CA-MPA. Additionally, all samples exhibit Coulombic efficiencies values close to 100 % during the 100 cycles (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0445">Fig. S5</ce:cross-ref>). Furthermore, surface-treated samples show improved iCE than C1400, that indicates the decrease of charge required for the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) (<ce:cross-ref refid="tbl3" id="crosref0450">Table 3</ce:cross-ref>). Additionally, to get a better insight into the effects of the ligands, samples without acid, C-L, were cycled under the same conditions at C/10 (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig3" id="crosref0455">Fig. 3</ce:cross-ref>d). C-MPA and C-EDT are the best treatments exhibiting 12 % and 6 % capacity improvement at 100th cycle respectively in comparison to pristine C1400. C-OxA shows a worse performance and stability. In terms of capacity, there are no differences between C-MPA, C-EDT and their acid counterparts but C-MPA and C-EDT show slightly better stability (93 % and 89 % capacity retention from the 2nd up to 100th cycle) (<ce:cross-ref refid="tbl3" id="crosref0460">Table 3</ce:cross-ref>). Furthermore, iCE is increased for the three treatments and Coulombic efficiencies are stable and close to 100 % during the 100 cycles of C-MPA and C-EDT (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0465">Fig. S5</ce:cross-ref>). In summary, most surface treatments have a positive effect on the electrochemical performance of C1400. Notably, MPA and EDT exhibit better performance, regardless of the presence or absence of acid. Additionally, the stability of the materials is enhanced.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0155" view="all">Additionally, galvanostatic cycling at 1C was also performed to identify the influence of the treatments at higher C-rates (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0470">Fig. S6</ce:cross-ref>) where hard carbons might show more limitations. The C1400 reference sample exhibits a decline in capacity after 55 cycles, most likely due to FEC consumption, whereas the surface-treated samples demonstrate enhanced stability, improved capacity and a delayed FEC consumption. Among them, C-L appears to be the most promising materials, with a notable emphasis on C-MPA and C-EDT with capacity improvements in the 100th cycle up to 13 % for C-MPA and 18 % for C-EDT and a capacity retention of 93 % for C-MPA and 95 % for C-EDT vs. 83 % for C1400 at higher C-rates (calculated from cycle 6 to 100).</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0160" view="all">Capacity, stability, and iCE enhancements are hypothesized to stem from differences in SEI formation and composition. This process is contingent upon the interactions between the surface groups of the carbon material and the electrolyte. The first Na charge–discharge curve is useful to see different SEI formation and to calculate iCE (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig3" id="crosref0475">Fig. 3</ce:cross-ref>b for CA-L and <ce:cross-ref refid="fig3" id="crosref0480">Fig. 3</ce:cross-ref>e for C-L samples). These curves represent the first discharge (sodiation)–charge (desodiation) cycle at C/10, between 2 V and 0 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup>. A typical curve shows a sloping region (attributed to the adsorption of Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> ions onto defects of the material) from high potentials up to 0.1 V and a plateau region below 0.1 V, attributed to the intercalation of Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> ions between carbon layers and pore filling by Na atoms. It is observed that in all samples the capacity improvement comes from both the slopping (above 0.1 V) and plateau (below 0.1 V) regions. To gain further insight, a detailed analysis of the inverse derivatives ((dV/dx)<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>) of this 1st cycle was conducted (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig3" id="crosref0485">Fig. 3</ce:cross-ref>c for CA-L samples and <ce:cross-ref refid="fig3" id="crosref0490">Fig. 3</ce:cross-ref>f for C-L samples). Two peaks can be distinguished corresponding to the SEI formation reactions [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib54" id="crosref0495">54</ce:cross-ref>]. In all samples, the first peak appears in discharge at around 1.1 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> and it is attributed to the decomposition of the FEC additive. To confirm this, the first cycle was performed in the same conditions without additive in electrolyte by using the C1400 sample (1 M NaPF<ce:inf loc="post">6</ce:inf> in EC:DMC). As can be seen in <ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0500">Fig. S7</ce:cross-ref>, the peak at 1.1 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> is not visible. Significant disparities are not observed in the initial peak among any of the samples, as it primarily depends on the presence of the FEC additive.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0165" view="all">Hence, the main focus is put on the second peak, which emerges between 0.6 V and 0.7 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup>. This peak is associated with EC and DMC decomposition and the formation of –CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>
                        <ce:sup loc="post">2-</ce:sup> species (polycarbonates, alkyl carbonates and Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>) [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib20" id="crosref0505">20</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib55" id="crosref0510">55</ce:cross-ref>]. Depending on the treatment applied, the peak appears at different voltage suggesting differences in the decomposition process (<ce:cross-ref refid="tbl3" id="crosref0515">Table 3</ce:cross-ref>) [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib56" id="crosref0520">56</ce:cross-ref>]. More precisely, the peak in CA-MPA and CA-EDT samples is less prominent and shifted to a higher voltage (0.73 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup>) which might suggest that Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf> and polycarbonate formation are less favorable (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig4" id="crosref0525">Fig. 4</ce:cross-ref>
                        <ce:float-anchor refid="fig4"/>c) [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib56" id="crosref0530">56</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib57" id="crosref0535">57</ce:cross-ref>]. On the other hand, CA and CA-OxA show lower voltage values (0.66 V and 0.67 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup>). We speculate that –CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>
                        <ce:sup loc="post">2-</ce:sup> formation could be enhanced by the presence of carboxylic groups, as discussed later in <ce:italic>ex-situ</ce:italic> XPS studies. Finally, two great peaks in the 0V–0.1 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> region are found in the discharge and charge processes, attributed to sodium insertion and de-insertion, respectively (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0540">Fig. S8</ce:cross-ref>). Non-acid pretreated samples (C-L, <ce:cross-ref refid="fig3" id="crosref0545">Fig. 3</ce:cross-ref>f) were examined, and similar correlations were identified. In C-MPA and C-EDT, the peak associated with solvent decomposition appears at higher voltages (0.7 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup>), whereas C-OxA exhibited the peak at 0.6 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup>, thus substantiating the previously drawn conclusions.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0170" view="all">Once the electrodes are stabilized, it is possible to differentiate between adsorption, intercalation and pore filing. The 100th charge–discharge curve and adsorption/insertion/pore filling column graphs were compared to clarify which step is most significantly influenced by surface treatments (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0550">Fig. S9</ce:cross-ref>). At both C-rates (C/10 and 1C) surface treatments seem to enhance both sodium insertion and adsorption. This effect is particularly noticeable in MPA and EDT treatments, especially at the higher rate of 1C, where HCs encounter more challenges.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0175" view="all">In summary, adding sulfuric acid to the surface treatment does not seem to provide any benefit regarding the amount of ligand loaded. Furthermore, the electrochemical results closely resemble those of the non-acid counterparts. Consequently, the primary focus of subsequent studies has been directed towards the C-L samples.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0180" view="all">To gain deeper insights into the underlying processes and the impact of each ligand on the electrochemical processes, N<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> adsorption and GITT measurements were conducted on C-L samples. Tuning the size or blocking the micropores entrance is a common strategy to avoid the undesired entry of solvated sodium ions into the pores [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib58" id="crosref0555">58</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib59" id="crosref0560">59</ce:cross-ref>]. If solvated ions enter the micropores, they can get trapped because of their large size. Therefore, blocking the pores reduces this loss. Moreover, the increase in the external surface area forces sodium storage through intercalation in the carbon matrix. Alternatively, if the micropore size is reduced, the sodium ions are forced to enter the pores without solvent molecules. Non-solvated ions enter and leave pores more easily leading to reversible storage of Na ions and improved iCE [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib25" id="crosref0565">25</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib26" id="crosref0570">26</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib60" id="crosref0575">60</ce:cross-ref>]. Our results suggest that short-chain ligands can partially block the material micropores increasing the carbon external surface area (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0580">Table S2</ce:cross-ref>). These data align with the improvement in the intercalation and the decrease in pore filling contribution, indicating a consistent trend in the observed results. GITT measurements (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0585">Fig. S10</ce:cross-ref>) were performed at C/10 with C1400 and C-L samples (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig4" id="crosref0590">Fig. 4</ce:cross-ref>) to calculate the apparent sodium diffusion coefficients (D) of the materials using the following formula:<ce:display>
                           <ce:formula id="ufd1">
                              <mml:math altimg="si1.svg">
                                 <mml:mrow>
                                    <mml:mi>D</mml:mi>
                                    <mml:mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mml:mo>
                                    <mml:mfrac>
                                       <mml:mn>4</mml:mn>
                                       <mml:mrow>
                                          <mml:mi>π</mml:mi>
                                          <mml:mi>τ</mml:mi>
                                       </mml:mrow>
                                    </mml:mfrac>
                                    <mml:msup>
                                       <mml:mrow>
                                          <mml:mo stretchy="true">(</mml:mo>
                                          <mml:mfrac>
                                             <mml:mrow>
                                                <mml:msub>
                                                   <mml:mi>m</mml:mi>
                                                   <mml:mi>B</mml:mi>
                                                </mml:msub>
                                                <mml:msub>
                                                   <mml:mi>V</mml:mi>
                                                   <mml:mi>M</mml:mi>
                                                </mml:msub>
                                             </mml:mrow>
                                             <mml:mrow>
                                                <mml:msub>
                                                   <mml:mi>M</mml:mi>
                                                   <mml:mi>B</mml:mi>
                                                </mml:msub>
                                                <mml:mi>S</mml:mi>
                                             </mml:mrow>
                                          </mml:mfrac>
                                          <mml:mo stretchy="true">)</mml:mo>
                                       </mml:mrow>
                                       <mml:mn>2</mml:mn>
                                    </mml:msup>
                                    <mml:msup>
                                       <mml:mrow>
                                          <mml:mo stretchy="true">(</mml:mo>
                                          <mml:mfrac>
                                             <mml:mrow>
                                                <mml:mo>Δ</mml:mo>
                                                <mml:msub>
                                                   <mml:mi>E</mml:mi>
                                                   <mml:mi>S</mml:mi>
                                                </mml:msub>
                                             </mml:mrow>
                                             <mml:mrow>
                                                <mml:mo>Δ</mml:mo>
                                                <mml:msub>
                                                   <mml:mi>E</mml:mi>
                                                   <mml:mi>t</mml:mi>
                                                </mml:msub>
                                             </mml:mrow>
                                          </mml:mfrac>
                                          <mml:mo stretchy="true">)</mml:mo>
                                       </mml:mrow>
                                       <mml:mn>2</mml:mn>
                                    </mml:msup>
                                 </mml:mrow>
                              </mml:math>
                           </ce:formula>
                        </ce:display>where <ce:italic>V</ce:italic>
                        <ce:inf loc="post">
                           <ce:italic>M</ce:italic>
                        </ce:inf> is the molar volume and <ce:italic>M</ce:italic>
                        <ce:inf loc="post">
                           <ce:italic>B</ce:italic>
                        </ce:inf> is the molar mass; both can be substituted by the inverse of the material density (1.973 g cm<ce:sup loc="post">−3</ce:sup> for C1400 [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib40" id="crosref0595">40</ce:cross-ref>], and 0.096 g cm<ce:sup loc="post">−3</ce:sup> for Vulcan, according to producer data). Same density values were used for their respective surface treated materials. <ce:italic>τ</ce:italic> is the relaxation time, <ce:italic>m</ce:italic>
                        <ce:inf loc="post">
                           <ce:italic>B</ce:italic>
                        </ce:inf> is the active mass in the electrode and <ce:italic>S</ce:italic> is the electrode area. <ce:italic>ΔE</ce:italic>
                        <ce:inf loc="post">
                           <ce:italic>s</ce:italic>
                        </ce:inf> corresponds to the steady-state voltage change and <ce:italic>ΔE</ce:italic>
                        <ce:inf loc="post">
                           <ce:italic>t</ce:italic>
                        </ce:inf> to the transient voltage change. The main assumptions are that the electrode material is homogeneous, the molar volume change of the active material during charge or discharge is not large enough and the current should be a low value with a short duration time.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0185" view="all">All samples exhibit similar D values which remain mostly constant during the sloping region. At 0.1 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup>, an abrupt decrease is observed due to the different mechanism of sodiation at the beginning of the plateau region. Afterward, D sharply increases displaying the typical U-shape of hard carbons to later decrease again near the cut-off voltage. C-OxA and especially C-MPA and C-EDT show a less pronounced decrease at 0.1 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> than C1400, which indicates that these materials would be less susceptible to polarization and display a better rate capability [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib61" id="crosref0600">61</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib62" id="crosref0605">62</ce:cross-ref>]. Indeed, C-L samples show better performance at 1C cycling than the untreated carbon (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0610">Fig. S6b</ce:cross-ref>). Moreover, D values of C-MPA and C-EDT in the plateau region (below 0.1 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup>) and near to the cut-off voltage are higher than C1400 and C-OxA ones. This indicates a faster diffusion of sodium during intercalation in the carbon matrix in C-MPA and C-EDT [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib61" id="crosref0615">61</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib62" id="crosref0620">62</ce:cross-ref>]. Furthermore, D values for desodiation cycle (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig4" id="crosref0625">Fig. 4</ce:cross-ref>b), exhibit similar tendencies. C-MPA and C-EDT have slightly higher D values in the intercalation region and a smoother transition into the adsorption mechanism at 0.12 V vs. Na/Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup>.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0190" view="all">Galvanostatic cycling tests at different C-rates for C-L samples was performed (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0630">Fig. S11</ce:cross-ref>). C-MPA and C-EDT demonstrate enhanced performance as compared to C1400 and C-OxA, with C-EDT showing slightly superior performance at higher C-rates. To better understand the involved processes, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were conducted (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0635">Fig. S12</ce:cross-ref>, <ce:cross-ref id="crosref081c0" refid="appsec1">Note S1</ce:cross-ref>). C-MPA and C-EDT exhibit significantly lower resistivity (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0640">Table S3</ce:cross-ref>) in the SEI region (85.6Ω and 58.5Ω) than C1400 and C-OxA (182.7Ω and 265.7Ω), indicating a more favorable Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> ion diffusion through the SEI. Moreover, these results point at a superior stability and electrochemical performance after the 40 cycles at different C-rates.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0195" view="all">Based on these results, it is clear that carbons treated with molecules containing –SH groups exhibit better performance, faster sodium diffusion in the plateau region, and improved micropore blockage [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib58" id="crosref0645">58</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib59" id="crosref0650">59</ce:cross-ref>]. It is probable that the presence of –COOH groups favors CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>
                        <ce:sup loc="post">2−</ce:sup> formation while –SH groups might lead to a more favorable SEI composition that directly affects capacity, stability, and iCE. For this reason, and to confirm the hypothesis, <ce:italic>ex-situ</ce:italic> XPS characterization of the electrodes has been conducted to identify and understand the SEI composition differences and their relationship with the functional groups of the organic ligands.</ce:para>
                  </ce:section>
                  <ce:section id="sec3.3" view="all">
                     <ce:label>3.3</ce:label>
                     <ce:section-title id="sectitle0080">Understanding SEI composition – <ce:italic>Ex-situ</ce:italic> XPS</ce:section-title>
                     <ce:para id="p0200" view="all">
                        <ce:italic>Ex-situ</ce:italic> XPS analysis after the first discharge–charge cycle enables the determination and quantification of the primary components and species of the SEI formed on the surface of the carbon electrode. In order to monitor NaF, –CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>
                        <ce:sup loc="post">2-</ce:sup>, Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O and organic species formation, special emphasis has been placed on C <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic>, O 1s and F <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> narrow spectra. All analyzed samples have been discharged (sodiated)–charged (desodiated) at C/10 to ensure an adequate formation of the SEI and make them comparable. The discussion focuses on C-L surface-treated samples, but similar results are observed for CA-L samples (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0655">Fig. S13</ce:cross-ref>). As compared to non-cycled materials (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig2" id="crosref0660">Fig. 2</ce:cross-ref>), in the C <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> narrow spectra (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig5" id="crosref0665">Fig. 5</ce:cross-ref>
                        <ce:float-anchor refid="fig5"/>a) two additional components at 283 eV and around 290 eV have been detected. The first one at 283 eV corresponds to sodium intercalated into the hard carbon Na<ce:inf loc="post">x</ce:inf>-HC [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib33" id="crosref0670">33</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib63" id="crosref0675">63</ce:cross-ref>]. This signal has been experimentally confirmed in literature through argon etching of the SEI [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib33" id="crosref0680">33</ce:cross-ref>], and can be used as an indicator of SEI thickness [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib63" id="crosref0685">63</ce:cross-ref>]. Considering that the maximum XPS penetration depth is approx. 10 nm, a more prominent Na<ce:inf loc="post">x</ce:inf>-HC signal in XPS could be correlated with a thinner SEI [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib64" id="crosref0690">64</ce:cross-ref>]. It has been reported that a thinner SEI can improve Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> ion transport [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib65" id="crosref0695">65</ce:cross-ref>]. The second signal at 290 eV corresponds to –CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>
                        <ce:sup loc="post">2-</ce:sup>, attributed to the formation of polycarbonates and Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf> in the SEI [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib53" id="crosref0700">53</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib63" id="crosref0705">63</ce:cross-ref>]. Furthermore, <ce:italic>ex-situ</ce:italic> O <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> peak fitting reveals several differences compared to the analyses conducted before cycling. The –COOH signal (534–535 eV) is not clearly detected; however, two additional components emerge, one at lower energy bindings (531 eV) attributed to Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O, and another at higher energy bindings (536 eV), representing the Na Auger signal (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig5" id="crosref0710">Fig. 5</ce:cross-ref>b). Besides, F <ce:italic>1s ex-situ</ce:italic> XPS fitting after the first discharge–charge display two components corresponding to the NaPF<ce:inf loc="post">6</ce:inf> electrolyte (687 eV) and the NaF (684 eV) formed in the SEI (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig5" id="crosref0715">Fig. 5</ce:cross-ref>c) [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib35" id="crosref0720">35</ce:cross-ref>,<ce:cross-ref refid="bib53" id="crosref0725">53</ce:cross-ref>]. For the following <ce:italic>ex-situ</ce:italic> XPS discussion, all the percentages correspond to atomic percentages and as commented, the focus will be on C <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> peak fitting (–CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>
                        <ce:sup loc="post">2-</ce:sup> and Na<ce:inf loc="post">x</ce:inf>-HC components), F <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> (NaF component) and O <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> (Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O). Results are summarized in <ce:cross-ref refid="tbl4" id="crosref0730">Table 4</ce:cross-ref>
                        <ce:float-anchor refid="tbl4"/> and element composition is compiled in <ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0735">Table S4</ce:cross-ref>.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0205" view="all">Starting with C <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic>, C1400 SEI has higher –CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>
                        <ce:sup loc="post">2-</ce:sup> content (16.0 %at.) than other samples. C-MPA and C-EDT, which have shown the most positive electrochemistry results, seem to have a thinner SEI (5.8 %at. and 6.4 %at. Na<ce:inf loc="post">x</ce:inf>-HC, respectively) with reduced amounts of –CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>
                        <ce:sup loc="post">2-</ce:sup> (12.8 %at. and 11.5 %at., correspondingly). On the other hand, C-OxA has similar values to C1400 and exhibits the worst performance. The correlation between the chemical SEI composition and the electrochemical behavior is evident. The presence of short-chain ligands and appropriate functional groups on the carbon surface promotes the formation of a thinner SEI and prevents unnecessary electrolyte decomposition (i.e., showing higher iCE values). This is clearly evidenced in the 1C electrochemistry of C-L and CA-L materials, where the sudden capacity fading attributed to FEC consumption, is notably delayed (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0740">Fig. S6</ce:cross-ref>). EDT and MPA treatments (both containing –SH groups) display a higher Na<ce:inf loc="post">x</ce:inf>-HC content, which has been reported to be associated with a thinner SEI, and have lower –CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>
                        <ce:sup loc="post">2-</ce:sup> content, which can result in higher ionic conductivity. On the other hand, the treatment with ligands containing only –COOH groups (C-OxA samples) promotes the formation of –CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>
                        <ce:sup loc="post">2-</ce:sup> which can hinder the performance.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0210" view="all">O <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> detailed spectra have been studied to gain insight into the inorganic compound Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig5" id="crosref0745">Fig. 5</ce:cross-ref>b). Peak fitting of C-MPA and C-EDT spectra demonstrate higher quantities of Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O (20.0 %at. and 16.9 %at., respectively) than C1400 (13.2 %at.) which can result in better stability and improved Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> conductivity. C-OxA components are very similar to C1400 results with lower amount of Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O (11.9 %at.) which explains its hindered performance. These results align with the previous C <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> conclusions.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0215" view="all">Furthermore, F <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> narrow spectra were analyzed (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig5" id="crosref0750">Fig. 5</ce:cross-ref>c). In this context, the NaF component stands out as particularly intriguing, given that a NaF-rich SEI has demonstrated advantageous effects such as better Na diffusion. Carbons treated with ligands: C-MPA (42.0 %at.), C-EDT (45.3 %at.) and C-OxA (33.9 %at.) show an increased amount of NaF content than C1400 (24.6 %at.) which can justify the enhanced electrochemistry of the C-L samples.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0220" view="all">In particular, carbons treated with thiol-containing molecules (MPA and EDT) show the highest content of Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O and NaF in their SEI, related to better cycling, Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> diffusion, and capacity retention. It is likely that the presence of thiol groups can promote the coordination of Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> on the surface of the electrode leading to an enrichment in sodium species (Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O and NaF) during the SEI formation. These results are consistent with the GCD, N<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> adsorption, GITT, and C <ce:italic>1s ex-situ</ce:italic> XPS conclusions.</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0225" view="all">As commented, C <ce:italic>1s,</ce:italic> F <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> and O <ce:italic>1s</ce:italic> spectra of samples treated in the presence of acid (CA-L) have also been analyzed and the conclusions are similar, with CA-MPA and CA-EDT showing the best results (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0755">Fig. S13</ce:cross-ref> and <ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0760">Table S5</ce:cross-ref>).</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0230" view="all">C-MPA and C-EDT treatments exhibited the highest specific capacities with excellent retention after 100 cycles (93 % and 89 %), blocked micropores, higher Na <ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> diffusion in the plateau region and a thinner SEI with increased amounts of Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O, NaF and lower amounts of –CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>
                        <ce:sup loc="post">2-</ce:sup>. It can be concluded that thiol-containing ligands without acid are the most beneficial surface treatments for the carbon anode in SIBs.</ce:para>
                  </ce:section>
                  <ce:section id="sec3.4" view="all">
                     <ce:label>3.4</ce:label>
                     <ce:section-title id="sectitle0085">Ligand treatments to soft carbon</ce:section-title>
                     <ce:para id="p0235" view="all">In light of the positive results observed with hard carbon treated with MPA and EDT, we decided exploring whether this surface treatment could enhance the performance of other carbon types used in commercial SIBs, such as soft carbons. For this reason, Vulcan XC-72R carbon black was treated with these molecules (V-MPA and V-EDT) and tested as anodes in SIBs half-cells. ATR-FTIR analysis of Vulcan, V-MPA, and V-EDT (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0765">Fig. S14</ce:cross-ref>) shows similar trends than observed for hard carbon (HC). The V-MPA spectrum shows a new signal at 1680 cm<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>, attributed to the C<ce:glyph name="dbnd"/>O vibration of the MPA carboxylic group. TGA analysis confirms the successful loading of MPA and EDT. Their curves show important mass losses within the range of chemical bonding (150 °C–400 °C) when compared to pristine Vulcan (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0770">Fig. S15</ce:cross-ref>). The total amount of ligands is 1 %wt. for both samples. The higher surface area of Vulcan allows for a slightly higher loading of organic molecules than C1400. Capacity improvements have been observed for both treatments during 100 cycles at C/10. As compared to pristine Vulcan XC-72R (136 mAh·g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup> at 100th cycle), V-MPA shows an 18 % capacity improvement (161 mAh·g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>) and V-EDT a 16 % increase (158 mAh·g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>) (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig6" id="crosref0775">Fig. 6</ce:cross-ref>
                        <ce:float-anchor refid="fig6"/>a). At a higher current, 1C, greater improvements are observed at the 100th cycle for both V-MPA and V-EDT. V-MPA showed a 23 % capacity improvement (155 mAh·g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>) and V-EDT 31 % (165 mAh·g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>) in comparison to Vulcan (126 mAh·g<ce:sup loc="post">−1</ce:sup>) (<ce:cross-ref refid="fig6" id="crosref0780">Fig. 6</ce:cross-ref>b).</ce:para>
                     <ce:para id="p0240" view="all">Additionally, N<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> adsorption shows a similar tendency where micropores are partially blocked with MPA while the external surface is promoted resulting in better and reversible insertion/deinsertion of sodium (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0785">Table S6</ce:cross-ref>). Apparent diffusion coefficients of Vulcan and V-MPA were also calculated at C/10 (<ce:cross-ref refid="appsec1" id="crosref0790">Fig. S16</ce:cross-ref>). The shape is consistent with a sloping region attributed to the adsorption of sodium on the surface, and the absence of U-shape, compatible with the reduced intercalation observed in soft carbons [<ce:cross-ref refid="bib66" id="crosref0795">66</ce:cross-ref>]. During the whole discharge (sodiation) process, V-MPA shows higher diffusion values, while they are similar for both materials (V-MPA and Vulcan) during charge (desodiation).</ce:para>
                  </ce:section>
               </ce:section>
               <ce:section id="sec4" role="conclusion" view="all">
                  <ce:label>4</ce:label>
                  <ce:section-title id="sectitle0090">Conclusions</ce:section-title>
                  <ce:para id="p0245" view="all">Surface treatments using various short-chain organic ligands (MPA, EDT OxA), both with and without acid, have been applied to hard carbon material and has been tested as anode in SIBs. The treatments have been beneficial for the electrode material showing excellent stability after 100 cycles and an improvement in capacity up to 12 % at C/10 and 18 % at 1C. Additionally, iCE is increased to 14 % in the best cases in comparison to the pristine material. Treatments with ligands incorporating thiol groups (C-MPA and C-EDT) have proven to be the most advantageous. The reasons behind these improvements have been studied using <ce:italic>ex-situ</ce:italic> XPS, N<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> adsorption, and GITT. Best performing materials show a thinner SEI with lower –CO<ce:inf loc="post">3</ce:inf>
                     <ce:sup loc="post">2-</ce:sup> amount and higher NaF and Na<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf>O content related to better Na diffusion, as confirmed by GITT measurements in the plateau region. N<ce:inf loc="post">2</ce:inf> adsorption measurements have shown micropore blocking and an increase of external surface area which seems to favor sodium insertion and extraction.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0250" view="all">Moreover, MPA and EDT treatments have also been applied to soft carbon Vulcan XC-72R and tested as the anode in SIBs half-cells. Capacity enhancement has been observed especially at higher charge–discharge rates (1C) showing more than a 30 % capacity improvement with great stability after 100 cycles. Again, micropore blocking and increased Na<ce:sup loc="post">+</ce:sup> diffusion seem to be responsible for this enhancement.</ce:para>
                  <ce:para id="p0255" view="all">In summary, we have shown that a simple, fast, low temperature treatment with short-chain organic ligands can improve the performance of carbon materials used as anodes in SIBs. Moreover, these treated-carbons show good performance at fast charge–discharge rates, which could be especially relevant for certain applications such as electric mobility.</ce:para>
               </ce:section>
               <ce:section id="sec5" view="all">
